<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">World population</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">The total number of living humans on Earth</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="Question book-new.svg" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">An editor has expressed concern that this article <b>may have a number of irrelevant <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">citations</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=World_population&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> these references and  <a href="/wiki/Template:Citation_needed" title="Template:Citation needed">challenge</a> any that are not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">reliable</a> or do not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">support the article</a>.</span> <small><i>(March 2016)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_population_v3.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="537" data-file-width="481" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/260px-World_population_v3.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/390px-World_population_v3.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/World_population_v3.svg/520px-World_population_v3.svg.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_population_v3.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World population estimates from 1800 to 2100, based on "high", "medium" and "low" <a class="external text" href="http://esa.un.org/wpp/unpp/panel_population.htm" rel="nofollow">United Nations projections in 2015</a>  and <a class="external text" href="https://esa.un.org/unpd/wpp/" rel="nofollow">UN historical estimates</a> for pre-1950 data</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="448" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png/260px-Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png/390px-Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/65/Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population_Growth_by_World_Bank_continental_division.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Population growth graph <a class="external text" href="https://www.google.co.uk/publicdata/explore?ds=d5bncppjof8f9_&amp;met_y=sp_pop_totl&amp;idim=country:GBR:FRA:DEU&amp;hl=en&amp;dl=en#!ctype=l&amp;strail=false&amp;bcs=d&amp;nselm=h&amp;met_y=sp_pop_totl&amp;scale_y=lin&amp;ind_y=false&amp;rdim=region&amp;idim=region:EAS:ECS:LCN:MEA:NAC:SAS:SSF&amp;ifdim=region&amp;tstart=-298602000000&amp;tend=1405465200000&amp;hl=en_US&amp;dl=en&amp;ind=false" rel="nofollow">generated</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_data" title="Public data">data</a> supplied by <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, via <a href="/wiki/Google" title="Google">Google</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_population_percentage.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2174" data-file-width="2006" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/World_population_percentage.png/260px-World_population_percentage.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/World_population_percentage.png/390px-World_population_percentage.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/World_population_percentage.png/520px-World_population_percentage.png 2x" width="260"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_population_percentage.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World population percentage by country</div></div></div>
<p>In <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographics" title="Demographics">demographics</a>, the <b>world population</b> is the total number of <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a> currently living, and was estimated to have reached 7.6 billion people as of May 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> It took over 200,000 years of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_history" title="Human history">human history</a> for the world's population to reach 1 billion;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> and only 200 years more to reach 7 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>World population has experienced <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">continuous growth</a> since the end of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%9317" title="Great Famine of 1315–17">Great Famine of 1315–17</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> in 1350, when it was near 370 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> 
The highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_growth_rates" title="Population growth rates">population growth rates</a> – global population increases above 1.8% per year – occurred between 1955 and 1975, peaking to 2.06% between 1965 and 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup> The growth rate has declined to 1.18% between 2010 and 2015 and is projected to decline further in the course of the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p><p>Total annual births were highest in the late 1980s at about 139 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and as of 2011 were expected to remain essentially constant at a level of 135 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> while deaths numbered 56 million per year and were expected to increase to 80 million per year by 2040.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> 
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Median_age" title="Median age">median age</a> of the world's population was estimated to be 30.4 years in 2018.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_by_region">Population by region</span></h2>
<p>Six of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s seven <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continents" title="Continents">continents</a> are permanently inhabited on a large scale. <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> is the most populous continent, with its 4.54 billion inhabitants accounting for 60% of the world population. The world's two most populated countries, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, together constitute about 36% of the world's population. <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> is the second most populated continent, with around 1.28 billion people, or 16% of the world's population. <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>'s 742 million people make up 10% of the world's population as of 2018, while the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_American" title="Latin American">Latin American</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> regions are home to around 651 million (9%). <a href="/wiki/Northern_America" title="Northern America">Northern America</a>, primarily consisting of the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>, has a population of around 363 million (5%), and <a href="/wiki/Oceania" title="Oceania">Oceania</a>, the least populated region, has about 41 million inhabitants (0.5%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Though it is not permanently inhabited by any fixed population, <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a> has a small, fluctuating international population based mainly in polar <a href="/wiki/Research_stations_in_Antarctica" title="Research stations in Antarctica">science stations</a>. This population tends to rise in the summer months and decrease significantly in winter, as visiting researchers return to their home countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AntarcticCIA_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-AntarcticCIA-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">World population estimates</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_world" title="History of the world">History of the world</a></div>
<p>Estimates of world population by their nature are an aspect of <a href="/wiki/Modernity" title="Modernity">modernity</a>, possible only since the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Discovery" title="Age of Discovery">Age of Discovery</a>. Early estimates for the population of the world<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup> date to the 17th century: <a href="/wiki/William_Petty" title="William Petty">William Petty</a> in 1682 estimated world population at  320 million (modern estimates ranging close to twice this number); by the late 18th century, estimates ranged close to one billion (consistent with modern estimates).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[15]</a></sup> More refined estimates, broken down by continents, were published in the first half of the 19th century, at 600 to 1000 million in the early 1800s and at 800 to 1000 million in the 1840s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is difficult for estimates to be better than rough approximations, as even modern population estimates are fraught with uncertainties on the order of 3% to 5%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_and_Post-classical_history">Ancient and Post-classical history</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Classical_demography" title="Classical demography">Classical demography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_demography" title="Medieval demography">Medieval demography</a></div>
<p>Estimates of the population of the world at the time agriculture emerged in around 10,000 BC have ranged between 1 million and 15 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[19]</a></sup>  Even earlier, genetic evidence suggests humans may have gone through a population bottleneck of between 1,000 and 10,000 people about 70,000 BC, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Toba_genetic_bottleneck" title="Lake Toba genetic bottleneck">Toba catastrophe theory</a>. By contrast, it is estimated that around 50–60 million people lived in the combined eastern and western <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> in the 4th century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Plague_of_Justinian" title="Plague of Justinian">Plague of Justinian</a>, which first emerged during the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justinian" title="Justinian">Justinian</a>, caused Europe's population to drop by around 50% between the 6th and 8th centuries AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[21]</a></sup> The population of Europe was more than 70 million in 1340.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[22]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> <a href="/wiki/Pandemic" title="Pandemic">pandemic</a> of the 14th century may have reduced the world's population from an estimated 450 million in 1340 to between 350 and 375 million in 1400;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[23]</a></sup> it took 200 years for population figures to recover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[24]</a></sup> The population of China decreased from 123 million in 1200 to 65 million in 1393,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[25]</a></sup> presumably due to a combination of <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol</a> invasions, famine, and plague.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Starting in AD 2, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_China" title="Ancient China">ancient China</a> kept consistent family registers in order to properly assess the poll taxes and labor service duties of each household.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nishijima_1986_pp595-96_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-nishijima_1986_pp595-96-30">[27]</a></sup> In that year, the population of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Han" title="Western Han">Western Han</a> was recorded as 57,671,400 individuals in 12,366,470 households, decreasing to 47,566,772 individuals in 9,348,227 households by AD 146, towards the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/End_of_the_Han_Dynasty" title="End of the Han Dynasty">End of the Han Dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nishijima_1986_pp595-96_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-nishijima_1986_pp595-96-30">[27]</a></sup> At the founding of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">Ming Dynasty</a> in 1368, China's population was reported to be close to 60 million; toward the end of the dynasty in 1644, it may have approached 150 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[28]</a></sup> England's population reached an estimated 5.6 million in 1650, up from an estimated 2.6 million in 1500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[29]</a></sup> New crops that were brought to Asia and Europe from the Americas by Portuguese and Spanish colonists in the 16th century are believed to have contributed to population growth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[32]</a></sup> Since their introduction to Africa by Portuguese traders in the 16th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[33]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">maize</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">cassava</a> have similarly replaced traditional African crops as the most important <a href="/wiki/Staple_food" title="Staple food">staple food</a> crops grown on the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pre-Columbian_era" title="Pre-Columbian era">pre-Columbian</a> North American population probably numbered somewhere between 2 million and 18 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[35]</a></sup> Encounters between European explorers and populations in the rest of the world often introduced local <a href="/wiki/List_of_epidemics" title="List of epidemics">epidemics</a> of extraordinary virulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[36]</a></sup> According to the most extreme scholarly claims, as many as 90% of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Population_history_of_American_indigenous_peoples" title="Population history of American indigenous peoples">Native American population</a> of the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a> died due to <a href="/wiki/Old_World" title="Old World">Old World</a> diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[37]</a></sup> Over the centuries, the Europeans had developed high degrees of immunity to these diseases, while the indigenous peoples had no such immunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history">Modern history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2006megacities.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="625" data-file-width="1425" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/2006megacities.svg/300px-2006megacities.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/2006megacities.svg/450px-2006megacities.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/2006megacities.svg/600px-2006megacities.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2006megacities.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing urban areas with at least one million inhabitants in 2006. Only 3% of the world's population lived in urban areas in 1800; this proportion had risen to 47% by 2000, and reached 50.5% by 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[39]</a></sup> By 2050, the proportion may reach 70%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[40]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>During the European <a href="/wiki/British_Agricultural_Revolution" title="British Agricultural Revolution">Agricultural</a> and <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolutions</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> of children increased dramatically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[41]</a></sup> The percentage of the children born in London who <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">died before the age of five</a> decreased from 74.5% in 1730–1749 to 31.8% in 1810–1829.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Buer_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Buer-45">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[43]</a></sup> Between 1700 and 1900, Europe’s population increased from about 100 million to over 400 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[44]</a></sup> Altogether, the areas populated by people of European descent comprised 36% of the world's population in 1900.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Population growth in the West became more rapid after the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Vaccination" title="Vaccination">vaccination</a> and other improvements in medicine and <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[46]</a></sup> Improved material conditions led to the population of Britain increasing from 10 million to 40 million in the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[47]</a></sup> The population of the United Kingdom reached 60 million in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> The United States saw its population grow from around 5.3 million in 1800 to 106 million in 1920, exceeding 307 million in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>The first half of the 20th century in <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Imperial Russia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> was marked by a succession of major wars, famines and other disasters which caused large-scale population losses (approximately 60 million excess deaths).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup> After the collapse of the Soviet Union, Russia's population declined significantly – from 150 million in 1991 to 143 million in 2012<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup> – but by 2013 this decline appeared to have halted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many countries in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">developing world</a> have experienced extremely rapid population growth since the early 20th century, due to economic development and improvements in public health. China's population rose from approximately 430 million in 1850 to 580 million in 1953,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup> and now stands at over 1.3 billion. The population of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, which was about 125 million in 1750, increased to 389 million in 1941;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[55]</a></sup> today, India, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> are collectively home to about 1.63 billion people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> had about 5 million inhabitants in 1815; its present-day successor, <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, now has a population of over 140 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[57]</a></sup> In just one hundred years, the population of Brazil decupled (x10), from about 17 million in 1900, or about 1% of the world population in that year, to about 176 million in 2000, or almost 3% of the global population in the very early 21st century. Mexico's population grew from 13.6 million in 1900 to about 112 million in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[59]</a></sup> Between the 1920s and 2000s, Kenya's population grew from 2.9 million to 37 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Milestones_by_the_billions">Milestones by the billions</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/World_population_milestones" title="World population milestones">World population milestones</a></div>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; float:right; clear:right; margin-left:8px; margin-right:0;">

<p>It is estimated that the world population reached one billion for the first time in 1804. It was another 123 years before it reached two billion in 1927, but it took only 33 years to reach three billion in 1960.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[61]</a></sup> Thereafter, the global population reached four billion in 1974, five billion in 1987, six billion in 1999 and, according to the United States Census Bureau, seven billion in March 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USCBcite_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-USCBcite-65">[62]</a></sup> The United Nations, however, estimated that the world population reached seven billion in October 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN7bn_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN7bn-66">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian7_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian7-67">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UPI7bn_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-UPI7bn-68">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to current projections, the global population will reach eight billion by 2024, and is likely to reach around nine billion by 2042. Alternative scenarios for 2050 range from a low of 7.4 billion to a high of more than 10.6 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldPopProspects_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-WorldPopProspects-69">[66]</a></sup> Projected figures vary depending on underlying statistical assumptions and the variables used in projection calculations, especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">fertility variable</a>. Long-range predictions to 2150 range from a population decline to 3.2 billion in the "low scenario", to "high scenarios" of 24.8 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WorldPopProspects_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-WorldPopProspects-69">[66]</a></sup> One extreme scenario predicted a massive increase to 256 billion by 2150, assuming the global fertility rate remained at its 1995 level of 3.04 children per woman; however, by 2010 the global fertility rate had declined to 2.52.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LongRangeProjections2003KeyFindings_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-LongRangeProjections2003KeyFindings-70">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fertility2010_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fertility2010-71">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is no estimation for the exact day or month the world's population surpassed one or two billion. The points at which it reached three and four billion were not officially noted, but the International Database of the United States Census Bureau placed them in July 1959 and April 1974 respectively. The United Nations did determine, and commemorate, the "Day of 5 Billion" on July 11, 1987, and the "Day of 6 Billion" on October 12, 1999. The Population Division of the United Nations declared the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Day_of_7_Billion" title="Day of 7 Billion">Day of 7 Billion</a>" to be October 31, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (October 2018)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_demographics">Global demographics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_the_world" title="Demographics of the world">Demographics of the world</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Expectancy_of_life.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="2754" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Expectancy_of_life.svg/400px-Expectancy_of_life.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Expectancy_of_life.svg/600px-Expectancy_of_life.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Expectancy_of_life.svg/800px-Expectancy_of_life.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Expectancy_of_life.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><table width="100%"> <tbody><tr> <td valign="top"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000CD; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> &gt;80</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #4169E1; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 77.5–80</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00BFFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 75–77.5</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #3CB371; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 72.5–75</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #32CD32; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 70–72.5</div> </td> <td valign="top"> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ADFF2F; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 67.5–70</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFFF00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 65–67.5</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FFD700; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 60–65</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF8C00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 55–60</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #FF4500; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> 50–55</div> </td></tr></tbody></table> 2015 map showing average life expectancy by country in years. In 2015, the World Health Organization estimated the average global life expectancy as 71.4 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOStats2016_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHOStats2016-73">[70]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>As of 2012, the global <a href="/wiki/Human_sex_ratio" title="Human sex ratio">sex ratio</a> is approximately 1.01 males to 1 female. The greater number of men is possibly due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gender_mortality_differentials" title="Gender mortality differentials">significant sex imbalances</a> evident in the Indian and Chinese populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndexWorldDemogr._74-0"><a href="#cite_note-IndexWorldDemogr.-74">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[72]</a></sup> Approximately 26.3% of the global population is aged under 15, while 65.9% is aged 15–64 and 7.9% is aged 65 or over.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndexWorldDemogr._74-1"><a href="#cite_note-IndexWorldDemogr.-74">[71]</a></sup> The median age of the world's population was estimated to be 29.7 years in 2014,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> and is expected to rise to 37.9 years by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>, the global average <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> is 71.4 years as of 2015, with women living an average of 74 years and men approximately 69 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHOStats2016_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHOStats2016-73">[70]</a></sup> In 2010, the global <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_rate" title="Fertility rate">fertility rate</a> was estimated at 2.52 children per woman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fertility2010_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fertility2010-71">[68]</a></sup> In June 2012, British researchers calculated the total weight of Earth's human population as approximately 287 million tonnes, with the average person weighing around 62 kilograms (137 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">CIA</a> estimated nominal 2013 <a href="/wiki/Gross_world_product" title="Gross world product">gross world product</a> at US$74.31 trillion, giving an annual global per capita figure of around US$10,500.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CIA_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-CIA-79">[76]</a></sup> Around 1.29 billion people (18.4% of the world population) live in <a href="/wiki/Extreme_poverty" title="Extreme poverty">extreme poverty</a>, subsisting on less than US$1.25 per day;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[77]</a></sup> approximately 870 million people (12.25%) are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Undernourished" title="Undernourished">undernourished</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> 83% of the world's over-15s are considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literacy_rate" title="Literacy rate">literate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndexWorldDemogr._74-2"><a href="#cite_note-IndexWorldDemogr.-74">[71]</a></sup> In June 2014, there were around 3.03 billion global Internet users, constituting 42.3% of the world population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Han_Chinese" title="Han Chinese">Han Chinese</a> are the world's largest single ethnic group, constituting over 19% of the global population in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> The world's most-spoken <a href="/wiki/First_language" title="First language">first languages</a> are <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin Chinese</a> (spoken by 12.44% of the world's population), Spanish (4.85%), English (4.83%), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a> (3.25%) and <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a> (2.68%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndexWorldDemogr._74-3"><a href="#cite_note-IndexWorldDemogr.-74">[71]</a></sup> The world's largest religion is <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, whose adherents account for 31% of the global population; <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> is the second-largest religion, accounting for 24.1%, and <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> the third, accounting for 13.78%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IndexWorldDemogr._74-4"><a href="#cite_note-IndexWorldDemogr.-74">[71]</a></sup> In 2005, around 16% of the global population were reported to be <a href="/wiki/Irreligion" title="Irreligion">non-religious</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Approximately 4.37 billion people live in these ten countries, representing around 57% of the world's population as of July 2018.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Most_densely_populated_countries">Most densely populated countries</span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fluctuation">Fluctuation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_population_growth" title="World population growth">World population growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">Population growth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Projections_of_population_growth" title="Projections of population growth">Projections of population growth</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_population_(UN).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1020" data-file-width="1150" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_%28UN%29.svg/300px-World_population_%28UN%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_%28UN%29.svg/450px-World_population_%28UN%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/World_population_%28UN%29.svg/600px-World_population_%28UN%29.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_population_(UN).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Estimates of population evolution in different <a href="/wiki/Continent" title="Continent">continents</a> between 1950 and 2050, according to the United Nations. The vertical axis is <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic</a> and is in millions of people.</div></div></div>
<p>Population size fluctuates at differing rates in differing regions. Nonetheless, population growth is the long-standing trend on all inhabited continents, as well as in most individual states. During the 20th century, the global population saw its greatest increase in known history, rising from about 1.6 billion in 1900 to over 6 billion in 2000. A number of factors contributed to this increase, including the lessening of the <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">mortality rate</a> in many countries by improved sanitation and <a href="/wiki/History_of_medicine#Modern_medicine" title="History of medicine">medical advances</a>, and a massive increase in agricultural productivity attributed to the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[95]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2000, the United Nations estimated that the world's population was growing at an annual rate of 1.14% (equivalent to around 75 million people),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[96]</a></sup> down from a peak of 88 million per year in 1989. By 2000, there were approximately ten times as many people on Earth as there had been in 1700. Globally, the population <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">growth rate</a> has been steadily declining from its peak of 2.19% in 1963, but growth remains high in Latin America, the Middle East, and <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="628" data-file-width="1357" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/300px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/450px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png/600px-Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fertility_rate_world_map_2.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of countries by fertility rate (2018), according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a></div></div></div>
<p>During the 2010s, Japan and some countries in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> began to encounter <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Negative_population_growth" title="Negative population growth">negative population growth</a> (i.e. a <a href="/wiki/Net_(economics)" title="Net (economics)">net</a> decrease in population over time), due to <a href="/wiki/Sub-replacement_fertility" title="Sub-replacement fertility">sub-replacement fertility</a> rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BigDecline_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-BigDecline-97">[92]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the United Nations stated that the rate of population growth was visibly diminishing due to the ongoing global <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a>. If this trend continues, the rate of growth may diminish to zero by 2050, concurrent with a world population plateau of 9.2 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[98]</a></sup> However, this is only one of many estimates published by the UN; in 2009, UN population projections for 2050 ranged between around 8 billion and 10.5 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[99]</a></sup> An alternative scenario is given by the statistician Jorgen Randers, who argues that traditional projections insufficiently take into account the downward impact of global urbanization on fertility. Randers' "most likely scenario" reveals a peak in the world population in the early 2040s at about 8.1 billion people, followed by decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[100]</a></sup> Adrian Raftery, a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a> professor of statistics and of sociology, states that "there’s a 70 percent probability the world population will not stabilize this century. Population, which had sort of fallen off the world’s agenda, remains a very important issue."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[101]</a></sup>
</p>

<p>Using the above figures, the change in population from 2010 to 2015 was:
</p>
<ul><li>World: +420 million</li>
<li>Africa: +142 million</li>
<li>Asia: +223 million</li>
<li>Europe: +3 million</li>
<li>Latin America and Caribbean: +35 million</li>
<li>Northern America: +14 million</li>
<li>Oceania: +2.9 million</li></ul>

<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-Americas-113"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Americas_113-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Americas_113-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Americas_113-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Americas_113-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Americas_113-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Americas_113-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><b>Northern America</b> comprises the northern-most countries and territories of North America: Canada, the United States, Greenland, <a href="/wiki/Bermuda" title="Bermuda">Bermuda</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Pierre_and_Miquelon" title="St. Pierre and Miquelon">St. Pierre and Miquelon</a>. <b>Latin America</b> comprises Mexico, Central America, the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, and South America.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Projections">Projections</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">World population estimates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Projections_of_population_growth" title="Projections of population growth">Projections of population growth</a></div>
<p>Long-term global population growth is difficult to predict. The United Nations and the US Census Bureau both give different estimates – according to the UN, the world population reached seven billion in late 2011,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-UN-107">[102]</a></sup> while the USCB asserted that this occurred in March 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[113]</a></sup> The UN has issued multiple projections of future world population, based on different assumptions. From 2000 to 2005, the UN consistently revised these projections downward, until the 2006 revision, issued on March 14, 2007, revised the 2050 mid-range estimate upwards by 273 million.
</p><p>Average global <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rates</a> are declining fast, but vary greatly between developed countries (where birth rates are often at or below replacement levels) and developing countries (where birth rates typically remain high). Different ethnicities also display varying birth rates. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death_rate" title="Death rate">Death rates</a> can change rapidly due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease#Mortality_from_infectious_diseases" title="Infectious disease">disease epidemics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_wars" title="List of wars">wars</a> and other mass catastrophes, or <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">advances in medicine</a>.
</p><p>2012 United projections show a continued increase in population in the near future with a steady decline in population growth rate; the global population is expected to reach between 8.3 and 10.9 billion by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[115]</a></sup> 2003 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_Nations_Population_Division" title="United Nations Population Division">UN Population Division</a> population projections for the year 2150 range between 3.2 and 24.8 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LongRangeProjections2003KeyFindings_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-LongRangeProjections2003KeyFindings-70">[67]</a></sup> One of many independent mathematical models supports the lower estimate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[116]</a></sup> while a 2014 estimate forecasts between 9.3 and 12.6 billion in 2100, and continued growth thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[118]</a></sup> Some analysts have questioned the sustainability of further world population growth, highlighting the <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">growing pressures on the environment</a>, global food supplies, and energy resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIMEenvir_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-TIMEenvir-126">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zehner_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zehner-127">[121]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align:center; margin:1em; float:left;">
<caption>UN (medium variant – 2012 revision) and US Census Bureau (June 2015) estimates<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UN12_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-UN12-128">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[123]</a></sup>
</caption>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mathematical_approximations">Mathematical approximations</span></h2>
<p>In 1975, <a href="/wiki/Sebastian_von_Hoerner" title="Sebastian von Hoerner">Sebastian von Hoerner</a> proposed a formula for population growth which represented <a href="/wiki/Hyperbolic_growth" title="Hyperbolic growth">hyperbolic growth</a> with an infinite population in 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[124]</a></sup> The hyperbolic growth of the world population observed until the 1970s was later correlated to a non-linear second order positive feedback between demographic growth and technological development. This feedback can be described as follows: technological advance → increase in the <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> of land for people → demographic growth → more people → more potential inventors → acceleration of technological advance → accelerating growth of the carrying capacity → faster population growth → accelerating growth of the number of potential inventors → faster technological advance → hence, the faster growth of the Earth's carrying capacity for people, and so on.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[125]</a></sup> The transition from hyperbolic growth to slower rates of growth is related to the <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">demographic transition</a>.
</p><p>According to the Russian demographer <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Kapitsa" title="Sergey Kapitsa">Sergey Kapitsa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kapitza_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kapitza-132">[126]</a></sup> the world population grew between 67,000 BC and 1965 according to the following formula:
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle N={\frac {C}{\tau }}\operatorname {arccot} {\frac {T_{0}-T}{\tau }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="N={\frac {C}{\tau }}\operatorname {arccot} {\frac {T_{0}-T}{\tau }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/dcd13b580792f3ef58830e4e394f97f0c7de8acb" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:22.469ex; height:5.343ex;"/></span></dd></dl>

<li><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \tau }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Years_for_world_population_to_double">Years for world population to double</span></h3>
<p>According to linear interpolation and extrapolation of <a href="/wiki/World_population_estimates" title="World population estimates">UNDESA population estimates</a>, the world population has doubled, or will double, in the years listed in the tables below (with two different starting points). During the <a href="/wiki/2nd_millennium" title="2nd millennium">2nd millennium</a>, each doubling took roughly half as long as the previous doubling, fitting the hyperbolic growth model mentioned above. However, after 2024, it is unlikely that there will be another doubling of the global population in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Population-doubling.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="950" height="221" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Population-doubling.svg/300px-Population-doubling.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Population-doubling.svg/450px-Population-doubling.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Population-doubling.svg/600px-Population-doubling.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Population-doubling.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Historic chart showing the periods of time the world population has taken to double, from 1700 to 2000</div></div></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Overpopulation">Overpopulation</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">Human overpopulation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malthusian_catastrophe" title="Malthusian catastrophe">Malthusian catastrophe</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predictions_of_scarcity">Predictions of scarcity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2718" data-file-width="3425" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg/280px-Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg/420px-Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg/560px-Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Los_Angeles_Aerial_view_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greater_Los_Angeles_Area" title="Greater Los Angeles Area">Greater Los Angeles</a> lies on a coastal mediterranean <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannah</a> with a small <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watershed</a> that is able to support at most one million people on <a href="/wiki/Water_scarcity" title="Water scarcity">its own water</a>; as of 2015, the area has a population of over 18 million. Researchers predict that similar cases of resource scarcity will grow more common as the world population increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeddWarn_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-BeddWarn-134">[128]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In his 1798 work <i><a href="/wiki/An_Essay_on_the_Principle_of_Population" title="An Essay on the Principle of Population">An Essay on the Principle of Population</a></i>, the British scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> incorrectly predicted that continued population growth would exhaust the global food supply by the mid-19th century. Malthus wrote the essay to refute what he considered the unattainable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Utopian" title="Utopian">utopian</a> ideas of <a href="/wiki/William_Godwin" title="William Godwin">William Godwin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marquis_de_Condorcet" title="Marquis de Condorcet">Marquis de Condorcet</a>, as presented in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_Justice" title="Political Justice">Political Justice</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Future_Progress_of_the_Human_Mind" title="The Future Progress of the Human Mind">The Future Progress of the Human Mind</a></i>. In 1968, <a href="/wiki/Paul_R._Ehrlich" title="Paul R. Ehrlich">Paul R. Ehrlich</a> reprised Malthus' argument in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Population_Bomb" title="The Population Bomb">The Population Bomb</a></i>, predicting that mass global <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a> would occur in the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>The predictions of Ehrlich and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-malthusianism" title="Neo-malthusianism">neo-Malthusians</a> were vigorously challenged by a number of economists, notably <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julian_Lincoln_Simon" title="Julian Lincoln Simon">Julian Lincoln Simon</a>, and advances in agriculture, collectively known as the <a href="/wiki/Green_Revolution" title="Green Revolution">Green Revolution</a>, forestalled any potential global famine in the late 20th century. Between 1950 and 1984, as the Green Revolution transformed agriculture around the world, grain production increased by over 250%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[130]</a></sup> The world population has grown by over four billion since the beginning of the Green Revolution, but food production has so far kept pace with population growth. Most scholars believe that, without the Revolution, there would be greater levels of famine and malnutrition than the UN presently documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[131]</a></sup> However, neo-Malthusians point out that <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> provided the energy for the Green Revolution, in the form of natural gas-derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertilizers" title="Fertilizers">fertilizers</a>, oil-derived <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pesticides" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>-fueled <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>, and that many crops have become so genetically uniform that a crop failure in any one country could potentially have global repercussions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[132]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2004, a meta-analysis of 70 quantitative studies estimating a <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">sustainable limit to the world population</a> generated a meta-estimate of 7.7 billion people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_den_BerghRietveld2004_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_den_BerghRietveld2004-139">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>In May 2008, the price of grain was pushed up severely by the increased cultivation of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>, the increase of world <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oil_prices" title="Oil prices">oil prices</a> to over $140 per barrel ($880/m<sup>3</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[134]</a></sup> global population growth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[135]</a></sup> the effects of <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[136]</a></sup> the loss of agricultural land to residential and industrial development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[138]</a></sup> and growing consumer demand in the population centres of China and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%932008_world_food_price_crisis" title="2007–2008 world food price crisis">Food riots</a> subsequently occurred in some countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup> However, oil prices then fell sharply. Resource demands are expected to ease as population growth declines, but it is unclear whether mass <a href="/wiki/Food_waste" title="Food waste">food wastage</a> and rising living standards in developing countries will once again create resource shortages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup>
</p><p>David Pimentel, professor of ecology and agriculture at <a href="/wiki/Cornell_University" title="Cornell University">Cornell University</a>, estimates that the sustainable agricultural carrying capacity for the United States is about 200 million people; its population as of 2015 is over 300 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[145]</a></sup> In 2009, the UK government's chief scientific advisor, Professor <a href="/wiki/John_Beddington" title="John Beddington">John Beddington</a>, warned that growing populations, falling energy reserves and food shortages would create a "perfect storm" of shortages of food, water, and energy by 2030.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BeddWarn_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-BeddWarn-134">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[146]</a></sup> According to a 2009 report by the United Nations <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organisation" title="Food and Agriculture Organisation">Food and Agriculture Organisation</a> (FAO), the world will have to produce 70% more food by 2050 to feed a projected extra 2.3 billion people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup>
</p><p>The observed figures for 2007 showed an actual increase in absolute numbers of undernourished people in the world, with 923 million undernourished in 2007, versus 832 million in 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup> The 2009 FAO estimates showed an even more dramatic increase, to 1.02 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impacts">Environmental impacts</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">Human impact on the environment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Manantenina_bushfire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Manantenina_bushfire.jpg/220px-Manantenina_bushfire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Manantenina_bushfire.jpg/330px-Manantenina_bushfire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Manantenina_bushfire.jpg/440px-Manantenina_bushfire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Manantenina_bushfire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Illegal <a href="/wiki/Slash-and-burn" title="Slash-and-burn">slash-and-burn</a> agriculture in <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>, 2010</div></div></div>
<p>A number of scientists have argued that the current global population expansion and accompanying increase in <a href="/wiki/Resource_consumption" title="Resource consumption">resource consumption</a> threatens the world's <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[151]</a></sup>
The <a href="/wiki/InterAcademy_Panel" title="InterAcademy Panel">InterAcademy Panel</a> <a href="/wiki/IAP_statement_on_population_growth" title="IAP statement on population growth">Statement on Population Growth</a>, which was ratified by 58 member <a href="/wiki/National_academy" title="National academy">national academies</a> in 1994, states that "unprecedented" population growth aggravates many environmental problems, including rising levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atmospheric_carbon_dioxide" title="Atmospheric carbon dioxide">atmospheric carbon dioxide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>, and pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[152]</a></sup> Indeed, some analysts claim that overpopulation's most serious impact is its effect on the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TIMEenvir_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-TIMEenvir-126">[120]</a></sup>
The situation has continued to worsen, as at the time of the 1994 IAP statement, the world population stood at 5.5 billion and lower-bound scenarios predicted a peak of 7.8 billion by 2050, a number that current estimates state will be reached in the late 2020s.
</p><p>Scientists contend that human overpopulation, continued human population growth and <a href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">overconsumption</a>, particularly by the wealthy, are the primary drivers of <a href="/wiki/Holocene_extinction" title="Holocene extinction">mass species extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extinctioncrisis_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-extinctioncrisis-161">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup> By 2050 population growth, along with profligate consumption, could result in oceans containing more <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic</a> than fish by weight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-extinctioncrisis_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-extinctioncrisis-161">[155]</a></sup> In November 2017, a <a href="/wiki/World_Scientists%27_Warning_to_Humanity" title="World Scientists' Warning to Humanity">statement</a> by 15,364 scientists from 184 countries asserted that rapid human population growth is the "primary driver behind many ecological and even societal threats."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[157]</a></sup>
</p><p>A July 2017 study published in <i><a href="/wiki/Environmental_Research_Letters" title="Environmental Research Letters">Environmental Research Letters</a></i> argued that the most significant way individuals could mitigate their own carbon footprint is to have fewer children, followed by living without a vehicle, forgoing air travel and adopting a plant-based diet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population_control">Population control</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_population_planning" title="Human population planning">Human population planning</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_(3059480968).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_%283059480968%29.jpg/220px-India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_%283059480968%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_%283059480968%29.jpg/330px-India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_%283059480968%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_%283059480968%29.jpg/440px-India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_%283059480968%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:India_-_Chennai_-_busy_T._Nagar_market_1_(3059480968).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>India is predicted to overtake China as the world's most populous country by 2022.</div></div></div>
<p>Human population control is the practice of intervening to alter the rate of population growth. Historically, human population control has been implemented by limiting a region's <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a>, by voluntary contraception or by government mandate. It has been undertaken as a response to factors including high or increasing levels of poverty, <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">environmental concerns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious reasons</a>. The use of <a href="/wiki/Abortion" title="Abortion">abortion</a> in some population control strategies has caused controversy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[159]</a></sup> with religious organizations such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> explicitly opposing any intervention in the human reproductive process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[160]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/University_of_Nebraska" title="University of Nebraska">University of Nebraska</a> publication <i>Green Illusions</i> argues that population control to alleviate environmental pressures need not be coercive. It states that "Women who are educated, economically engaged, and in control of their own bodies can enjoy the freedom of bearing children at their own pace, which happens to be a rate that is appropriate for the aggregate ecological endowment of our planet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup> The book <i>Fatal Misconception</i> by Matthew Connelly similarly points to the importance of supporting the rights of women in bringing population levels down over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The population of the world, which Sir W. P. in 1682, stated at only 320 millions, has been estimated by some writers at about 730 millions, by others, at upwards of 900 millions; Mr. Wallace, of Edinburgh, conjectured it might amount to 1000 millions, and this number has since generally been adopted who have noticed the subject;" <i>The Monthly Magazine</i> 4 (July–December 1797), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=0S0AAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA167#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 167</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">600 million: Simon Gray, <i>The Happiness of States</i> (1818), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ue5eAAAAcAAJ&amp;pg=PA356" rel="nofollow">p. 356</a>.
800 million:  Gordon Hall, Samuel Newell, <i>The Conversion of the World</i> (1818), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=ZzA3AAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA10" rel="nofollow">p. 10</a>.
800 to 1000 million: John Redman Coxe, <i>Considerations Respecting the Recognition of Friends in Another World</i> (1845), <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Gl9IAAAAYAAJ&amp;pg=PA21#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false" rel="nofollow">p. 21</a> (footnote with references).</span>

<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Even recent demographic data is accurate only from 3 to 5%, although in demography traditionally more digits are indicated than those having a meaning. This is partially due to the ethical difficulty in rounding off numbers that supposedly represent real people, officially counted during a census." Sergei P Kapitza, 'The phenomenological theory of world population growth', <i>Physics-Uspekhi</i> 39(1) 57-71 (1996).</span>

<li id="cite_note-162"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-162">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Ceballos, Gerardo; Ehrlich, Paul R; Dirzo, Rodolfo (23 May 2017). <a class="external text" href="http://www.pnas.org/content/early/2017/07/05/1704949114.full" rel="nofollow">"Biological annihilation via the ongoing sixth mass extinction signaled by vertebrate population losses and declines"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Proceedings_of_the_National_Academy_of_Sciences_of_the_United_States_of_America" title="Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences of the United States of America">PNAS</a></i>. <b>114</b>: 201704949. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1073%2Fpnas.1704949114" rel="nofollow">10.1073/pnas.1704949114</a>. <a href="/wiki/PubMed_Central" title="PubMed Central">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC5544311" rel="nofollow">5544311</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/28696295" rel="nofollow">28696295</a>. <q>Much less frequently mentioned are, however, the ultimate drivers of those immediate causes of biotic destruction, namely, human overpopulation and continued population growth, and overconsumption, especially by the rich. These drivers, all of which trace to the fiction that perpetual growth can occur on a finite planet, are themselves increasing rapidly.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=PNAS&amp;rft.atitle=Biological+annihilation+via+the+ongoing+sixth+mass+extinction+signaled+by+vertebrate+population+losses+and+declines&amp;rft.volume=114&amp;rft.pages=201704949&amp;rft.date=2017-05-23&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC5544311&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F28696295&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1073%2Fpnas.1704949114&amp;rft.aulast=Ceballos&amp;rft.aufirst=Gerardo&amp;rft.au=Ehrlich%2C+Paul+R&amp;rft.au=Dirzo%2C+Rodolfo&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.pnas.org%2Fcontent%2Fearly%2F2017%2F07%2F05%2F1704949114.full&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWorld+population"></span><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/></span>
